[Read] /workspace/problems/06_sonic_moe_swiglu/PROMPT.txt
tool
1	I need you to write the up-projection of a top-K MoE FFN with grouped GEMM and fused SwiGLU for the RTX PRO 6000 (SM120 Blackwell, GDDR7, 1.8 TB/s). The reference implementation is in reference.py and your kernel goes in solution.py. You can make whatever mess you want in this directory — scratch files, intermediate kernels, build artifacts, profiling traces — but the final answer has to be in solution.py with the same Model, get_inputs, and get_init_inputs interface as reference.py.
2	
3	The op is: per expert e, compute h_e = silu(x_e @ W_gate[e]) * (x_e @ W_up[e]) where x_e is the slice of permuted hidden states routed to expert e. Inputs are hidden_states of shape (T_perm, H) bf16 (already permuted into expert order, T_perm = T_total * K), expert_offsets of shape (E+1,) int32 (prefix sums of token counts per expert, so expert e owns rows [offsets[e]:offsets[e+1]]), and per-expert weights W_gate and W_up each of shape (E, H, I) bf16. You return gated_up of shape (T_perm, I) bf16. Correctness tolerance is 0.02 abs/rel on bf16. The shapes you have to handle are T_total=32768 H=4096 I=1536 E=128 K=8 (the headline sonic-moe configuration, 32K tokens, 128 experts, top-8), T_total=4096 H=2048 I=1024 E=64 K=4 (smaller / faster iteration), and T_total=16384 H=2048 I=4096 E=64 K=8 (intermediate-heavy aspect ratio that forces larger N tiles).
4	
5	This needs to be a real custom kernel — the whole point is to implement the variable-length grouped GEMM and fuse SwiGLU into the epilogue, not to dispatch to a vendor library. Don't import or call torch.matmul, torch.bmm, torch.nn.functional.linear, F.linear, or anything from sonic_moe. Try CUTLASS grouped GEMM (the intended path), Triton with a grouped-GEMM autotune, CUDA C++ via load_inline, or inline PTX — your call. Anything you're uncertain about, look up PTX docs, clone CUTLASS or sonic-moe or quack-kernels or other reference repos, read library headers, and investigate.
6	
7	Your flywheel is implement, profile (ncu, nsys, torch.profiler — whatever's useful) and time it with benchmark.py, verify correctness by running `python check.py` and reading the output, then iterate. Don't substitute your own one-off correctness snippets for check.py — it iterates over every shape, your spot-check almost certainly won't. If `python check.py` hasn't printed PASS, you're not done. Take as long as you need to actually push the number up.
8

reasoning
None of these have BF16 SM120 MMA. This is a problem because we need BF16. Let me think about this differently. Actually wait - SM120 RTX PRO 6000 is consumer Blackwell. Looking at PTX docs, SM120 still supports the standard BF16 MMA instructions. The shape is typically m16n8k16 or m16n8k32. But many MMAs are 16x8x16 fp16/bf16. Let me check the PTX docs to confirm. Actually, the standard Blackwell instruction is mma.sync.aligned.m16n8k16.row.col.f32.bf16.bf16.f32. SM120 inherits Hopper-style wmma/MMA support. We can use these. So the strategy: 1. Use Triton's `tl.dot` which handles SM120 BF16 MMA automatically. 2. Or use inline PTX with `mma.sync.aligned.m16n8k16.row.col.f32.bf16.bf16.f32`. Triton is easier and likely gives close to optimal performance. Let me start with Triton. But first, let me check if Triton is configured for SM120.
assistant

Let me analyze: - Shape 0 (big): 26.5 TFLOPs / 200 = 13.25% peak - Shape 1 (small): 22.2 TFLOPs / 200 = 11.09% peak - Shape 2 (intermediate): 26.3 TFLOPs / 200 = 13.13% peak - Geomean: 12.45% peak OK so we're at 12.45% of peak. We need to push this much higher. The pass threshold is 0.1, but we should aim for >50% peak for a respectable score. Let me think about what's making us slow: 1. **Tile sizes**: I picked BLOCK_M=128, BLOCK_N=128, BLOCK_K=32. These are conservative. Let me try larger. 2. **Two dots per K iteration**: We're doing two `tl.dot` per K iteration (one for gate, one for up). This may not be optimal because the MMA pipeline gets longer. The right way is to load W as (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) and do a single dot. 3. **Block table building**: We do a CPU sync to build the block table. This is slow! Let me move it to GPU. 4. **Concatenation**: We concat W_gate and W_up on every forward pass. This is a 2x W copy every time. Let me cache the concatenated W. 5. **Numerical accumulation**: We're accumulating in FP32, which is correct for BF16. Let me work on optimizations one by one. **Optimization 1: Avoid the W concatenation** We can precompute W = cat(W_gate, W_up) once in __init__ if the weights don't change. But nn.Parameter can be modified... Actually the safest is to concat lazily and cache. Or we can do the cat only if it's not cached. Actually, the issue is that load_state_dict will modify W_gate and W_up after init. We need to recompute the concat. The most efficient way: store W as a buffer and update it before each forward, or use a pre-concat format. Wait, I notice the model has load_state_dict called. The benchmark calls load_state_dict with strict=True. So we need to handle re-loading. Approach 1: Use a buffer for W, recompute in forward only if W_gate/W_up have changed. Approach 2: Modify the kernel to take W_gate and W_up separately. Approach 3: Always concat in forward (current approach). Approach 3 has a 2x memory bandwidth cost. For a 6 GB problem, the W is 2*2*I*E*H = 2*2*1536*128*4096 = 3.2 GB, and reading+writing the concat is 6.4 GB. So the cat is a 1.6 ms overhead by itself. For the small shape, the W is 0.5 GB, and the cat is 0.28 ms. Let me restructure: have a cached W that we update in-place if needed, or skip the cat and use two separate weights. Actually, the best is to make the kernel take W_gate and W_up directly. The kernel can compute the gate and up pointers separately without a physical concat. Let me try this: ```python @triton.jit def _grouped_gemm_swiglu_kernel( A_ptr, W_gate_ptr, W_up_ptr, Out_ptr, block_to_expert_ptr, block_to_m_abs_ptr, H, N, # N=I stride_am, stride_ak, stride_we, stride_wk, stride_wn, stride_om, stride_on, BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K, ): ... w_gate_ptrs = ( W_gate_ptr + e * stride_we + offs_k[:, None] * stride_wk + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wn ) w_up_ptrs = ( W_up_ptr + e * stride_we + offs_k[:, None] * stride_wk + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wn ) ... ``` This is the cleanest. No concat needed. **Optimization 2: Move block table to GPU** Currently we do `expert_offsets.cpu()` to build the table. This is a sync. For shapes with E=128 and ~256 blocks per expert, this could be 32k entries. The sync is the killer. Solution: build the block table on GPU using a tiny kernel. The build is: 1. From offsets[E+1], compute counts[E] = offsets[1:] - offsets[:-1] 2. Compute block count per expert: (counts + BLOCK_M - 1) // BLOCK_M 3. Compute prefix sum of block counts 4. Use a kernel to fill in the block table Actually, the simplest approach is to use a small CUDA kernel that directly fills the block table. Or, even simpler: launch with (max_m_blocks, num_n_blocks) grid and do the in-kernel search. Wait, actually the in-kernel search using a precomputed inverse mapping is fast. Let me think about this: For each m_block, we want to know the expert. The mapping is monotonic (block i is for expert e where e is such that m_abs[i] falls in [offsets[e], offsets[e+1])). Approach: precompute a device-side lookup table. This is one tiny device operation per forward, but no sync. Actually, let me think about it differently. The block table only depends on the offsets, which are passed at forward time. If we can compute it on the device, we avoid the CPU sync. **Approach: GPU block table builder** Use a single kernel to build the block table. Or, use `torch.cumsum` to compute prefix sums of block counts and use that. But the simplest is: just use a Python list comprehension that runs on the GPU. Wait, that doesn't work. OK, let me think about the in-kernel search. The standard approach: For each m_block, we want to find e such that offsets[e] <= m_abs < offsets[e+1]. In Triton, we can do this with a binary search (since the offsets are sorted). With E=128, log2(128) = 7 iterations. Alternative: use a 1-element lookup: precompute for each m_block index its expert id. This is the table approach. For the table approach, we need the table to be on the device. The cleanest is to build it on the device: ```python def _build_block_table_gpu(expert_offsets, E, BLOCK_M, T_perm, device): # counts[E] counts = expert_offsets[1:] - expert_offsets[:-1] # blocks_per_expert[E] blocks_per_expert = (counts + BLOCK_M - 1) // BLOCK_M # prefix sum: total_blocks total_blocks = blocks_per_expert.sum().item() # sync # cumulative: cumsum of blocks_per_expert expert_block_offsets = torch.zeros(E, dtype=torch.int32, device=device) expert_block_offsets[1:] = torch.cumsum(blocks_per_expert, dim=0)[:-1] # Now fill the table: # For each block index b in [0, total_blocks), find which expert it belongs to. # Use a kernel. block_to_expert = torch.empty(total_blocks, dtype=torch.int32, device=device) block_to_m_abs = torch.empty(total_blocks, dtype=torch.int32, device=device) # Kernel: for each b, find e and m_abs _build_block_table_kernel[(total_blocks,)](...) return block_to_expert, block_to_m_abs ``` Hmm, but this still has a sync (total_blocks = ...item()). The cleanest is to size the grid to a known max and exit early: ```python def _grouped_gemm_swiglu(...): # max possible blocks max_m_blocks = (T_perm + BLOCK_M - 1) // BLOCK_M grid_m = max_m_blocks # over-provision # In the kernel, each program checks if it's a real block ... ``` But this wastes work for unbalanced experts. For our balanced case, all experts have exactly T_perm/E tokens, and T_perm/E is divisible by BLOCK_M=128 for all shapes. So max_m_blocks = T_perm/128 = sum of (n_e/128) for all e. So we can pre-compute this on the device. Wait, let me check: T_perm = 262144 for shape 0, BLOCK_M=128, so max_m_blocks = 2048. Sum of n_e/128 over all experts = (sum of n_e)/128 = 262144/128 = 2048. So for balanced experts, max_m_blocks = sum of (n_e/128) = T_perm/128. So we can just launch a grid of T_perm/128 m-blocks. For each m-block: - m_abs = pid_m * BLOCK_M - Find expert: binary search or linear search over offsets For E=128, binary search is 7 iterations. That's fine. Even simpler: precompute an inverse mapping on the device. Use a single small kernel. Actually, here's a clever trick: we can compute the block table on the device using a small kernel that's parallelized over the m_blocks. The number of m_blocks is known: T_perm / BLOCK_M (for balanced). For each m_block, we know the m_abs. We need to find the expert. This can be done with a single small kernel that does a binary search over the offsets. But honestly, the CPU sync is probably only ~1ms and the GPU work is 30+ms. So it's not the bottleneck. Let me focus on the kernel itself first. **Optimization 3: Use 2*BLOCK_N in the dot** Currently we do two dots: one for gate (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) and one for up (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N). Instead, load (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) and do one dot. This: - Halves the number of dot instructions - Increases the dot size, which is usually more efficient ```python b = tl.load(w_ptrs) # (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) acc = tl.dot(a, b, acc=acc) # (BLOCK_M, 2*BLOCK_N) # Split for SwiGLU # In Triton, this is tricky. We can either: # 1. Use the result directly and do silu * up # 2. Store gate and up separately # Option 1: We have a (BLOCK_M, 2*BLOCK_N) accumulator. We need to: # - Take first BLOCK_N cols, apply silu # - Take last BLOCK_N cols # - Multiply # - Store the result # In Triton, we can do this with reshape: acc_reshaped = tl.reshape(acc, (BLOCK_M, 2, BLOCK_N)) # Then acc_reshaped[:, 0, :] is gate and acc_reshaped[:, 1, :] is up # Or use a strided slice gate = tl.load... # not possible ``` Hmm, splitting inside a Triton kernel is awkward. Let me think... Actually, we can keep two separate accumulators and load the (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) tiles separately. The two-dot approach might actually be more efficient because: - Each dot has independent MMA pipelining - The two W tiles are loaded in parallel Let me try the merged approach first, then compare. **Actually, the optimal pattern for this in Triton is:** Use `tl.dot` with a (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) B tensor. This produces a (BLOCK_M, 2*BLOCK_N) accumulator. Then we need to apply silu(gate) * up. The way to do this in Triton: 1. Keep two separate accumulators, load two B tiles. (current approach) 2. Use a single accumulator of shape (BLOCK_M, 2*BLOCK_N), and split via reshape. Let me try option 2: ```python b = tl.load(b_ptrs) # (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) acc = tl.dot(a, b, acc=acc) # (BLOCK_M, 2*BLOCK_N) # In epilogue, we have acc of shape (BLOCK_M, 2*BLOCK_N) # We want to do silu(first half) * second half # In Triton, this is: acc_2d = acc # shape (BLOCK_M, 2*BLOCK_N) gate = acc_2d[:, :BLOCK_N] # this might not work ``` Triton supports slicing on the last dim, I believe. Let me check. Actually, the standard approach in Triton is to keep two accumulators and apply silu and mul separately. This is what flashinfer and similar libraries do. Let me stick with the two-accumulator approach. **Optimization 4: Larger tiles** The SM120 has more shared memory. Let me try: - BLOCK_M=128, BLOCK_N=256, BLOCK_K=64 Or: - BLOCK_M=64, BLOCK_N=128, BLOCK_K=64 (with K=4096 and H=2048, we can do 64 K iterations) **Optimization 5: Pipeline depth** num_stages=3 is moderate. Try num_stages=4 or 5 for more pipelining. **Optimization 6: Use TMA / cp.async** Triton 3.6 supports these on SM90+. SM120 should support them too. The `tl.dot` should automatically use TMA. **Optimization 7: Persistent kernel** Launch fewer programs (one per SM) and have each process multiple tiles. This reduces launch overhead and improves cache reuse. But for our shapes, the launch overhead is probably not the bottleneck. **Let me focus on the biggest wins first:** 1. Remove the W concatenation (saves bandwidth and a copy) 2. Try larger tile sizes 3. Autotune over configs Let me restructure the solution to use W_gate and W_up directly.

Help on function split in module triton.language.core:

split(a, _semantic=None, _generator=None) -> 'tuple[tensor, tensor]'
    Split a tensor in two along its last dim, which must have size 2.
    
    For example, given a tensor of shape (4,8,2), produces two tensors of shape
    (4,8).  Given a tensor of shape (2), returns two scalars.
    
    If you want to split into more than two pieces, you can use multiple calls
    to this function (probably plus calling reshape).  This reflects the
    constraint in Triton that tensors must have power-of-two sizes.

+        fn(*inputs)

+    s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)

+    for _ in range(n_iter):

The two-dot approach: - Load A (BM, BK) = 8KB - Load W_gate (BK, BN) = 8KB - Load W_up (BK, BN) = 8KB - Two dots The merged approach: - Load A (BM, BK) = 8KB - Load W (BK, 2*BN) = 16KB - One dot of (BM, 2*BN) - Split and SwiGLU The merged approach has the same memory traffic but 1 dot instead of 2. Each dot is (BM, BK) @ (BK, 2*BN) vs (BM, BK) @ (BK, BN). The merged is 2x larger. The issue is splitting. Let me try `tl.split` on a reshaped tensor. Wait, the dot output is (BM, 2*BN) in row-major. To split, I need to: - Reshape to (BM, BN, 2) -- this is just a view, no data movement - Then `tl.split` on the last dim of size 2 - Result: two (BM, BN) tensors The reshape (BM, 2*BN) -> (BM, BN, 2) reorders the data: - Original: row i has elements [a_0, a_1, ..., a_{BN-1}, b_0, b_1, ..., b_{BN-1}] - Reshaped: row i has shape (BN, 2) where [a_j, b_j] is at position (j, :) So tl.split would give: - First (BM, BN) tensor: a_0, a_1, ..., a_{BN-1} (gate) - Second (BM, BN) tensor: b_0, b_1, ..., b_{BN-1} (up) But wait, the original dot output is (BM, 2*BN) where columns 0..BN-1 are gate and BN..2*BN-1 are up. So: - Original: row i has [gate_0, gate_1, ..., gate_{BN-1}, up_0, up_1, ..., up_{BN-1}] - Reshape to (BM, BN, 2): row i has (j, 0) = gate_j, (j, 1) = up_{BN+j} -- wait, this is wrong Let me re-think. If the original is (BM, 2*BN) and we reshape to (BM, BN, 2), then: - For row i and column (j, 0): original position is (i, j*2 + 0) = gate_j - For row i and column (j, 1): original position is (i, j*2 + 1) = up_j Wait, that's gate and up interleaved! But I want gate in first half and up in second half. Hmm, so: - Original (BM, 2*BN): row i = [gate_0, gate_1, ..., gate_{BN-1}, up_0, up_1, ..., up_{BN-1}] - Reshape to (BM, BN, 2): row i, j=0..BN-1: [gate_j, up_j] -- this is interleaved - Reshape to (BM, 2, BN): row i, 0=gate, 1=up -- this is what I want So I need to reshape to (BM, 2, BN) which has last dim BN, not 2. `tl.split` won't work on this. To use `tl.split`, I'd need to first transpose to put BN first, then have last dim 2. Or permute (BM, 2, BN) -> (BM, BN, 2). But the last dim is now 2, and `tl.split` gives: - (BM, BN) tensor: result[i, j] = orig[i, j, 0] = gate_j (after my (BM, 2, BN) reshape) - (BM, BN) tensor: result[i, j] = orig[i, j, 1] = up_j (after my (BM, 2, BN) reshape) So the path is: 1. dot gives (BM, 2*BN) where col 0..BN-1 = gate, col BN..2*BN-1 = up 2. reshape to (BM, 2, BN) -- last dim is BN 3. transpose to (BM, BN, 2) -- last dim is 2 4. tl.split -- gives two (BM, BN) tensors The transpose is an extra op. Triton should be able to do this in registers (no shared memory), so it's free in terms of memory but adds instructions. Actually, let me check if `tl.trans` does this. The signature is `tl.trans(input, dims)` where dims is the new order. For (BM, 2, BN) -> (BM, BN, 2), dims = (0, 2, 1). Let me try this. Or even simpler: use `tl.reshape` directly. The reshape is a "view" so it doesn't move data, just changes the interpretation. The issue is that the "view" requires the memory layout to match. Triton handles this by inserting a copy or using a new register layout. For the case of (BM, 2*BN) -> (BM, 2, BN), the layout changes: - (BM, 2*BN): element at (i, j) for j in [0, 2*BN) - (BM, 2, BN): element at (i, k, l) for k in [0, 2), l in [0, BN) In memory, if both are row-major: - (BM, 2*BN): row i starts at offset i*2*BN, elements are [a_{i,0}, a_{i,1}, ..., a_{i,2*BN-1}] - (BM, 2, BN): row i starts at offset i*2*BN, elements are [b_{i,0,0}, b_{i,0,1}, ..., b_{i,0,BN-1}, b_{i,1,0}, ..., b_{i,1,BN-1}] For the layouts to be the same, we need a_{i,j} = b_{i, j//BN, j%BN}. So: - b_{i, 0, 0} = a_{i, 0}, b_{i, 0, 1} = a_{i, 1}, ..., b_{i, 0, BN-1} = a_{i, BN-1} - b_{i, 1, 0} = a_{i, BN}, b_{i, 1, 1} = a_{i, BN+1}, ..., b_{i, 1, BN-1} = a_{i, 2*BN-1} So if my original (BM, 2*BN) has gate at [0, BN) and up at [BN, 2*BN), then: - b_{i, 0, l} = gate_l (first half of 2*BN) ✓ - b_{i, 1, l} = up_l (second half of 2*BN) ✓ So reshape (BM, 2*BN) -> (BM, 2, BN) is a valid view. Now I want to split this into two (BM, BN) tensors. `tl.split` requires last dim = 2. So I need to transpose (BM, 2, BN) -> (BM, BN, 2). Transpose in Triton: `tl.trans(x, (0, 2, 1))`. After transpose, the last dim is 2. `tl.split` gives: - First (BM, BN): c_{i, j, 0} = b_{i, 0, j} = gate_j - Second (BM, BN): c_{i, j, 1} = b_{i, 1, j} = up_j So we get gate and up. Let me implement this. Actually, an even simpler approach: just use a strided store. We can compute silu(acc[:, 0:BN]) and acc[:, BN:2*BN], then store to two separate output buffers. But we'd need to slice acc. In Triton, slicing the last dim of a 2D tensor of size 2*BN into two slices of size BN is done with `tl.split` after a transpose, or with a custom load pattern. Actually, let me try another approach: do the silu and mul using a custom kernel that operates on the (BM, 2*BN) tensor and writes (BM, BN) output directly. ```python # acc is (BM, 2*BN) # We want: out[i, j] = silu(acc[i, j]) * acc[i, j + BN] # # Trick: build a "stacked" view. acc has columns [gate_0, ..., gate_{BN-1}, up_0, ..., up_{BN-1}] # We want to compute silu on first half and up on second half. # # Use a mask: mask_gate = (col < BN), mask_up = (col >= BN) # silu_full = acc * sigmoid(acc) # silu applied to BOTH halves (wrong for up) # # Hmm, this doesn't work because silu(up) != up. # # Alternative: compute silu on the gate part only by zeroing out the up part: # silu_masked = tl.where(mask_gate, acc * sigmoid(acc), 0) # silu(gate) at first half, 0 at second half # # Then we need to multiply silu(gate) (at first half) with up (at second half). # # But the multiplication is at (i, j) = (i, j) for j in [0, BN) -- we want silu(gate[j]) * up[j]. # We have: # - silu_masked[i, j] = silu(gate_j) for j in [0, BN), 0 for j in [BN, 2*BN) # - acc[i, j] = gate_j for j in [0, BN), up_j for j in [BN, 2*BN) # # out[i, j] = silu_masked[i, j] * acc[i, j + BN] for j in [0, BN) # = silu(gate_j) * up_j (for j in [0, BN)) ✓ # # To do this multiplication: we can shift acc by BN columns. # shift: shifted_acc[i, j] = acc[i, j + BN] = up_j for j in [0, BN) # We need shifted_acc of shape (BM, BN). # # shifted_acc = acc[:, BN:2*BN] -- but we can't slice. ``` This is getting complicated. Let me just try the tl.split approach. Actually, here's an even simpler approach: use the fact that we have a (BM, 2*BN) accumulator. We can do: ```python # Apply silu to first BN columns of acc # Multiply by up values (which are in columns BN..2*BN-1) # # Build a 2D tensor where for each (i, j) in [0, BN), we have: # result[i, j] = silu(acc[i, j]) * acc[i, j + BN] # # One way: # Compute shifted_up[i, j] = acc[i, j + BN]. This is the "up" half. # Compute silu_gate[i, j] = silu(acc[i, j]). This is the "gate" half (silu'd). # Then result = silu_gate * shifted_up. # # To get shifted_up: tl.load... no, we need to access acc[i, j+BN] in registers. # This requires either: # 1. Reshape + transpose + split # 2. Mask + shift ``` OK let me just try the tl.split approach. It's the cleanest. Let me check the SASS to see if there are any obvious issues. Actually, let me try a different angle: pre-compute the W in a transposed form. The W is (E, H, I). When we do A @ W[e], the contraction is over H. We can pre-transpose W to (E, I, H), then load as (BK, BN) tiles where the W's H dim is the K dim. This makes the W load more contiguous. Actually, the current layout has W[e, h, i] = W_ptr + e*H*I + h*I + i. Loading a (BK, BN) tile = (64, 64) starting at (h_start, i_start) means: - For thread (k, n), load W[e, h_start + k, i_start + n] - Memory: W_ptr + e*H*I + (h_start + k)*I + (i_start + n) - For adjacent n: offset is +1. So adjacent threads in n read adjacent memory. ✓ If we transpose to W_T[e, i, h] = W_ptr_T + e*I*H + i*H + h, then loading (BK, BN) where BK is the K dim and BN is the H dim: - For thread (k, n), load W_T[e, i_start + n, h_start + k] - Memory: W_T_ptr + e*I*H + (i_start + n)*H + (h_start + k) - For adjacent n: offset is +H. So adjacent threads in n read memory that is H apart. Not contiguous. So the current W layout (E, H, I) is actually GOOD for our access pattern. The K dim is the second dim of W (H), and N is the third (I). Threads in n are contiguous. OK so the W layout is fine. Let me think about what else could be slow. In Triton 3.6, TMA is available via `tl.experimental.descriptor` or similar. Let me check. Actually, the standard Triton `tl.dot` already uses TMA on SM90+ for the operand loads. Actually, looking at the merged-dot test, the issue is that the diff is 0.22, which is much larger than 0.02. Something is fundamentally wrong with the split. Let me trace through more carefully. With the 2D version (BM, 2*BN), the path is: 1. dot gives (BM, 2*BN) where col 0..BN-1 = gate, col BN..2*BN-1 = up 2. reshape (BM, 2*BN) -> (BM, 2, BN) -- this is just a view 3. transpose (0, 2, 1) -> (BM, BN, 2) 4. split -> (BM, BN) and (BM, BN) The issue might be in step 3: `tl.trans` might not support specifying a permutation for 3D tensors. Or the reshape might not be a view. Let me try a different approach: don't merge the dots. Use two separate dots but load B as a single (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) tile and use it for both. To split, I can use a mask on the load. Actually, the issue with masking is that we'd waste half the dot work. Let me think differently. OK here's a different approach. Load the (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) tile once. Then do two dots: - dot(a, b[:, 0:BN]) for gate - dot(a, b[:, BN:2*BN]) for up To do this in Triton, I need to slice the second dim of a 2D tensor. Let me check if Triton supports this. In Triton, slicing the last dim of a 2D tensor is done with arange-based masks. But for `tl.dot`, the B tensor needs to be of a specific shape. Actually, `tl.dot` accepts a 2D B tensor of shape (K, N). If I have a (K, 2N) B tensor, I can't directly slice it. But I can do: ```python b = tl.load(b_ptrs) # (K, 2N) # Reshape to (K, 2, N) b_r = tl.reshape(b, (BLOCK_K, 2, BLOCK_N)) # Transpose to (K, N, 2) b_t = tl.trans(b_r, (0, 2, 1)) # Split b_g, b_u = tl.split(b_t) # (K, N), (K, N) ``` This is the same trick but on the B tile, not the accumulator. Let me see if this is faster. Actually, the dot of (BM, K) @ (K, N) with K=64, N=64, BM=128 produces a 128*64 = 8K element accumulator. Splitting this in registers is doable. The dot of (BM, K) @ (K, 2N) with K=64, N=64, BM=128 produces a 128*128 = 16K element accumulator. Same size, just different shape. Splitting should be similar. The benefit: one tl.dot vs two tl.dot. The mma instruction count is the same (since 2N = 2*64 = 128 in the merged case, same as 2 dots of 64). But the dot loop body is shorter, so less instruction issue pressure. The merged approach also loads the W as one tile (16KB) instead of two tiles (8KB each). This might be slightly better for memory access. OK let me just try the merged version with the correct split logic. Let me first verify the split logic works on a simple example.

But the diff was 0.22. Let me think... Oh wait! The issue is that the dot output layout is NOT necessarily (BM, 2*BN) in row-major. The MMA instruction has its own output layout. The "logical" shape might be (BM, 2*BN) but the physical register layout is determined by the MMA. When we reshape, Triton might need to do a register shuffle to convert from MMA layout to row-major. If it doesn't, the reshape is incorrect. Actually, this is a known issue with Triton. The dot output layout doesn't match the standard row-major layout, so operations like reshape that assume row-major are incorrect. In the test above, I used a manually loaded tensor (via tl.load), not a dot output. So the layout was correct. But the dot output has a different layout. So the fix: use a different approach. We need to get the dot output into row-major form before splitting. One way: write the dot output to shared memory and read it back in row-major. But that's expensive. Another way: keep two separate accumulators. The separate-dot approach is correct because each accumulator is the output of a single dot, and the layout is internally consistent. OK so the merged-dot approach is tricky. Let me stick with the separate-dot approach. Wait, let me also try the variant where we split the B tile, not the accumulator: ```python b = tl.load(b_ptrs) # (BK, 2*BN) # Reshape (BK, 2*BN) -> (BK, 2, BN) b_r = tl.reshape(b, (BLOCK_K, 2, BLOCK_N)) # Transpose to (BK, BN, 2) b_t = tl.trans(b_r, (0, 2, 1)) # Split b_g, b_u = tl.split(b_t) # (BK, BN), (BK, BN) # Use in dots acc_g = tl.dot(a, b_g, acc=acc_g) acc_u = tl.dot(a, b_u, acc=acc_u) ``` The B tile is loaded via tl.load, so it has row-major layout. Reshape, transpose, split should work. But this might not be faster than two separate loads, because the two loads are already efficient. OK let me just stick with the separate-dot approach and focus on other optimizations. Let me look at what else can be done: 1. The kernel is at 40 TFLOPs (20% peak). Need to push higher. 2. The two-dot approach: per K iter, we load 2 B tiles and do 2 dots. Total per K iter: 2 dots + 3 loads. Alternative: load 1 B tile (size 2*BN) and do 1 dot. Then apply silu to the first half and multiply by the second half (which requires splitting). The split issue: the dot output has a specific register layout. We need to get it into a layout where the first BN columns and last BN columns are in separate tensors. In Triton, the way to do this is via `tl.split` after a transpose, but the dot output layout may not match what `tl.split` expects. Let me try a different approach: use `tl.split` directly on the dot output. The dot output of (BM, K) @ (K, 2*BN) might be in a layout where the last dim is naturally split-able. Actually, the dot output of (BM, K) @ (K, 2*BN) where 2*BN is even... the output is a (BM, 2*BN) tensor. To split into two (BM, BN), I can use: - `tl.reshape((BM, 2, BN))` to get (BM, 2, BN) - Then `tl.trans((0, 2, 1))` to get (BM, BN, 2) - Then `tl.split` on the last dim of 2 to get (BM, BN) and (BM, BN) But the dot output layout might be different from row-major. The reshape would need to convert layouts. Hmm, let me look at how Triton handles dot output layouts. Actually, the dot output is in the layout determined by the MMA. For Hopper, the m16n8k16 MMA has output in a specific layout. Triton abstracts this away with `tl.dot` returning a "regular" tensor, but the underlying layout is MMA-specific. When you do `tl.reshape` on a dot output, Triton may need to do a layout conversion. This could be expensive. The cleanest solution: keep two separate accumulators, and accept the cost of two dots. OR: use the merged dot approach but write the result to shared memory first, then read it back in row-major form, then split. Peak is 200 TFLOPs for the WHOLE GPU. Per SM: 200/188 = 1.06 TFLOPs. Per SM work: 33 GFLOPs. Time at peak: 33 / 1.06 TFLOPs = 33 / 1060 GFLOPs/s = 0.031 ms = 31 us. But we measure 21 ms = 21000 us. So per SM, work / time = 33 GFLOPs / 21 ms = 1.57 TFLOPs/SM. That's higher than 1.06 TFLOPs/SM peak. So we're getting more than peak per SM. That's impossible. My math is wrong somewhere. Let me redo. Total FLOPs for shape 0 = 824 GFLOPs (from problem.yaml). Time = 21 ms = 0.021 s. Achieved TFLOPs = 824 / 0.021 / 1000 = 39.2 TFLOPs. That's 39.2 / 200 = 19.6% of peak. Per SM, achieved = 39.2 / 188 = 0.208 TFLOPs. Time per SM = 0.021 s. Work per SM = 0.208 * 0.021 = 4.4 GFLOPs. So per SM, 4.4 GFLOPs of work in 21 ms. At 1.06 TFLOPs/SM peak, this would take 4.4 / 1.06 = 4.2 ms. So we're 5x slower than peak. The work per SM is 4.4 GFLOPs, and the theoretical time is 4.2 ms. We measure 21 ms. So 5x slower. This matches: 21 ms / 4.12 ms theoretical = 5.1x slower. ✓ So we're 5x slower than theoretical peak. Why? Possibilities: 1. The MMAs are stalled waiting for memory (most likely) 2. The dot loops are not pipelined well 3. The shared memory is full and we're spilling For BM=128 BN=64 BK=64 with 4 warps and num_stages=3: - A per stage: 128*64*2 = 16KB - B_gate per stage: 64*64*2 = 8KB - B_up per stage: 64*64*2 = 8KB - Per stage: 32KB - 3 stages: 96KB (just fits in 96KB shared mem) - Per thread (4 warps = 128 threads): 32KB / 4 = 8KB per warp / 32 = 256 bytes per thread for accumulators - Wait, accumulators are in registers not shared mem. Accumulators: - 2 accumulators of (128, 64) fp32 = 8192 fp32 = 32KB per accumulator - 2 accumulators = 64KB - Per warp (32 threads): 64KB / 4 = 16KB / 32 = 512 bytes per thread - In fp32 registers: 128 registers per thread for accumulators So we have 128 registers per thread for accumulators. SM120 has up to 255 registers per thread. So we're fine. Hmm, so register pressure isn't the issue. The bottleneck must be memory access. Let me look at the achieved memory bandwidth: - Total memory traffic: 5.8 GB (assuming perfect L2 reuse) - Time: 21 ms - Achieved bandwidth: 5.8 / 21 * 1000 = 276 GB/s = 15% of 1.8 TB/s So we're at 15% of peak bandwidth. The DRAM is the bottleneck (we're not saturating it). Why aren't we saturating DRAM? Possible reasons: 1. Not enough memory parallelism (few in-flight loads) 2. Bad access pattern (uncoalesced) 3. Bank conflicts in shared memory Let me look at the access pattern. For the W tile (BK, BN) = (64, 64) bf16, we load from W[e, k:k+BK, n:n+BN]. The W is (E, H, I) row-major with stride (H*I, I, 1). Loading (BK, BN) is a contiguous block of BK*BN = 4096 bf16 = 8KB. The inner dim is the I dim, which is contiguous. So adjacent threads access adjacent memory. Coalesced. ✓ For the A tile (BM, BK) = (128, 64) bf16, we load from A[m:m+BM, k:k+BK]. The A is (T_perm, H) row-major with stride (H, 1). Loading (BM, BK) is a contiguous block of BM*BK = 8192 bf16 = 16KB. The inner dim is the H dim, which is contiguous. So adjacent threads (in the n direction) access adjacent memory. But in Triton, the A tile is (BM, BK), so threads in the BK dim access adjacent memory. Coalesced. ✓ So access pattern is fine. The issue might be: not enough in-flight loads. With num_stages=3, we have 3 K-loop iterations in flight. Each iter loads A, B_gate, B_up = 3 tiles. So 9 tiles in flight per program. Each tile is 8KB-16KB. Total in-flight memory: 9 * 16 = 144KB per program. With 188 SMs, total in-flight = 188 * 144KB = 27 MB. The DRAM has bandwidth 1.8 TB/s, so to saturate we need many in-flight requests. 27 MB / 1.8 TB/s = 15 us. So our 15 us "request pipeline" should be saturated. But we measure 21 ms = 21000 us. So we're not even close to saturating. The issue must be that the MMAs are stalled. The MMAs can't start until the loads complete, and the loads are slow because of low concurrency. To increase concurrency, we can: 1. Use more num_stages (limited by shared mem) 2. Use larger tiles (limited by registers/shared mem) 3. Run more programs per SM (limited by SM resources) Let me check: with 4 warps per program, the SM can have multiple programs. SM120 has 1536 max threads per SM, 32 max blocks per SM. With 4 warps = 128 threads per program, we can have 12 programs per SM. If we have 12 programs per SM, the total programs = 188 * 12 = 2256. With 49152 tiles, each program does 49152/2256 = 22 tiles. That's a lot less per program. With non-persistent kernel and 2256 programs, the CUDA scheduler can run them in parallel. With persistent kernel and 188 programs, each does more work. The non-persistent kernel might give better load balancing because the scheduler can swap programs when one stalls. Let me try the non-persistent kernel with multiple programs per SM. Actually, the current "persistent" kernel still launches 188 programs. The difference is that each program does multiple tiles. If we launch more programs, the CUDA scheduler can do better load balancing. Let me try a non-persistent kernel with more programs.

reasoning
Writing a complete CUDA kernel with proper mma.sync instructions is a significant effort. Let me focus on what I can do quickly. Let me look at the solution once more and make sure it's good. We're at 22.4% peak. Let me also try one more thing: use the W concat with proper handling. Actually, the merged dot has a known issue with layout. Let me look at how to fix it properly. The dot output of (BM, K) @ (K, 2*BN) has shape (BM, 2*BN). The "natural" interpretation is row-major. But the MMA produces a different layout. For m16n8k16 MMA: the output of one MMA is 16*8 = 128 fp32 values. These are distributed across 32 threads (1 warp), 4 fp32 per thread. For a (BM, 2*BN) = (128, 128) dot, we have 8 (M) * 16 (N) = 128 MMAs. With 4 warps, each warp does 32 MMAs. The 128 MMAs are arranged as 8 M tiles and 16 N tiles, with each M tile being 16 rows and each N tile being 8 cols. The output layout: thread (warp, lane) holds acc[warp_m][warp_n] where warp_m is in [0, 8) and warp_n is in [0, 16). For 4 warps, the 4 warps handle different (warp_m, warp_n) ranges. When we do `tl.reshape` on this layout, Triton may need to do a register shuffle to convert to row-major. Actually, in Triton, the dot output is in "tensor" form, which Triton abstracts from the physical layout. The reshape should work correctly IF the dot's output is logically a (BM, 2*BN) tensor. But the issue is that the split requires the output to be in a specific layout where the last dim can be split. Let me try one more approach: do TWO dots, but with the B tile loaded as a single (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) tile, and split it via tl.split before the dot. The B tile is loaded via tl.load, which gives a (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) tensor in row-major layout. Reshape to (BLOCK_K, 2, BLOCK_N) and split into two (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) tensors should work. Let me try this.

reasoning
The split_b is both slower AND wrong. The diff is 0.21 for shape 0. So splitting the B tile via reshape+transpose+split is incorrect. The issue: when we load (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) and reshape to (BLOCK_K, 2, BLOCK_N), then transpose to (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N, 2), then split, the resulting tensors are INTERLEAVED, not contiguous halves. The original (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) tile has: - row 0: [w_g[0,0], w_g[0,1], ..., w_g[0,BN-1], w_u[0,0], w_u[0,1], ..., w_u[0,BN-1]] After reshape to (BLOCK_K, 2, BLOCK_N): - (k, 0, l) = original[k, l] for l in [0, BN) = w_g[k, l] - (k, 1, l) = original[k, l + BN] for l in [0, BN) = w_u[k, l] After transpose (0, 2, 1) to (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N, 2): - (k, l, 0) = (k, 0, l) = w_g[k, l] - (k, l, 1) = (k, 1, l) = w_u[k, l] After split (last dim of size 2): - First tensor: w_g (k, l) = original (k, l) for l in [0, BN) - Second tensor: w_u (k, l) = original (k, l + BN) for l in [0, BN) So the split should give w_g as the first BN cols and w_u as the last BN cols. That's what we want! But the result is wrong. Why? Oh, I think the issue is that the dot output layout is different from what tl.dot expects as input. When we do `tl.dot(a, w_g)` where w_g came from a tl.split of a loaded tensor, the layout might not match. Hmm, let me check more carefully. The B tensor from a tl.load is in row-major layout. The reshape + transpose + split might preserve this. Wait, the split requires last dim of size 2. After reshape (BLOCK_K, 2, BLOCK_N) -> transpose -> (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N, 2). The last dim is 2. Split gives (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) and (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N). The resulting tensors are in some layout. When passed to tl.dot, the compiler may need to do a layout conversion. If the layout conversion is incorrect, the result would be wrong. Let me try a simpler approach: just use two separate loads (which is the current approach and works correctly). The "split the B tile" approach has correctness issues. OK the merged approach with split B doesn't work. Let me stop trying to merge and focus on making the current approach faster.
